SECTION B - Supplies or Services/Prices B.1 BRIEF DESCRIPTION OF SERVICES "Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators (IV)" The overall purpose of this procurement is to provide Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators (QI) Program 2011-2016 to ensure the continuity, timeliness, and accuracy of the core tasks of the AHRQ Quality Indicator project. This includes providing general user support; technical measurement support of the AHRQ QIs; clinical knowledge of the measures, support of the QI website and related activities; enhancement, refinement, and updating of current software programs; assisting stakeholders with QI implementation; evaluation of impact; provision of administrative and management support to AHRQ; and integration of QI research and development work within the user and technical support systems. See Section C for a complete description.

B.2 TYPE OF CONTRACT This is a cost-plus-fixed-fee contract.

B.3 SEVERABLE SERVICES The services acquired under this contract are severable services. Funds are only available for use for the line item to which they are obligated. Unused funds from one period (line item) may not rollover for use in other periods. B.4 CONSIDERATION AND PAYMENT (CPFF) This is a cost-plus fixed-fee contract. In consideration of the satisfactory performance of the work as described in Section C, the contractor will be compensated in accordance with the following schedule below. The period of performance is for a 12-month base period plus four 12-month option periods.

SECTION C - Description/Specifications
PROJECT TITLE: SUPPORT FOR QUALITY INDICATORS IV

C.1 STATEMENT OF WORK The Contractor shall furnish all of the necessary personnel, materials, services, facilities, (except as otherwise specified herein), and otherwise do all the things necessary for or incident to the performance of the work as set forth below: The Center for Delivery, Organization, and Markets (CDOM) within the Agency for Healthcare Research and Quality (AHRQ) of the United States Department of Health and Human Services (DHHS) requires the maintenance and support of the AHRQ Quality IndicatorsTM (QIs) (2014-2019). The objectives of this statement of work are threefold: - maintain scientific integrity of technical specifications and software for use in health care reform - transition of the AHRQ QIs to both an electronic health record (EHR) and ICD10 environment, - provide technical assistance to users of the AHRQ QIs To meet these objectives, the following activities are required: 1) Maintain scientific integrity of the AHRQ QIs 2) Transition the AHRQ QIs to an ICD-10 environment 3) Support the transition of the AHRQ QIs to an EHR environment 4) Prepare timely, uniformly formatted AHRQ QI documentation, including technical specifications and technical documents 5) Maintain customer-oriented, streamlined AHRQ QI software to facilitate user's calculation of AHRQ QI rates 6) Review and provide recommendations for the AHRQ QI Web site, which serves as the project's primary vehicle for public outreach, and virtual repository for project information and documentation 7) Deliver customer-oriented technical support on AHRQ QIs technical documentation, software and products developed for the AHRQ QI project 8) Maintain and support customer-oriented communication with AHRQ QI users

Page 10 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

This focus is in response to the Health Information Technology for Economic and Clinical Health (HITECH) Act, Title XIII of Division A and Title IV of Division B of the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act of 2009 (ARRA) (Pub. L. 111­5) which was enacted in 2009 to improve health care quality, safety, and efficiency through the promotion of Health Information Technology (HIT) and electronic health information exchange. Under HITECH, several AHRQ QIs are included in the Centers for Medicare & Medicaid Services (CMS) EHR incentive programs thereby necessitating retooling of the AHRQ measures and maintaining these measures in an EHR environment. In October 2014, ICD-10 will be the required coding standard by all programs supported within the Department of Health and Human Services (HHS). These overlapping requirements have significant impact on the AHRQ QI methodology and maintenance processes which need to be addressed concurrently.

BACKGROUND INFORMATION C.1.1. AHRQ Quality Indicators C.1.1.1 Mission and Vision AHRQ is the federal agency tasked with producing evidence to make health care safer, higher quality, more accessible and more affordable, and to work with HHS and other partners to make sure that the evidence is understood and used. One of the basic foundations for producing evidence on how to improve quality is quality measurement. As 1 of 12 agencies within the DHHS, AHRQ supports research and tools that help people make informed decisions and that improve the quality of health care services. AHRQ, along with other DHHS agencies, participates in the Affordable Care Act's National Quality Strategy (NQS), a nationwide effort to align public and private interests to improve the quality of health and health care for all Americans. NQS is guided by three aims: 1) to provide better and more patient-centered, reliable, accessible and safe care, 2) to improve the health of the US population and 3) to reduce the cost of quality health care for individuals, families, employers and the government (US DHHS, 2013; US DHHS, 2011). One way AHRQ carries out its mission and vision is to provide researchers, data organizations, health care planning administrators, hospital executives, purchasers, insurers, local and state agencies, and federal agencies with the tools to assess the quality of care being provided in hospitals. AHRQ also used data on hospital admissions to provide information about potentially preventable admissions, to help health departments, accountable care organizations (ACOs), Medicaid agencies, and others target primary care and public health efforts. Quality measures, such as the AHRQ QIs, are widely used for research, health care planning, quality improvement, and reporting initiatives throughout the United States (US).

Page 11 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

C.1.1.2 History of the AHRQ Quality Indicators In 1994, staff at the Agency for Health Care Policy and Research (AHCPR), now called the AHRQ, developed the original Quality Indicators as part of the Healthcare Cost and Utilization Project (HCUP). These original indicators were created in response to requests from State partners who contributed data to HCUP and from researchers seeking to understand health care quality using administrative data. HCUP Partners and researchers sought approaches to make better use of their hospital administrative data, but did not necessarily have the technical expertise and resources to develop measures de novo. In 2000, the Agency embarked upon a process to expand, refine and severity-adjust the HCUP QIs through the Evidence-based Practice Center (EPC) at University of California-San Francisco and Stanford University. This effort resulted in the development of the AHRQ QIs, the current state of the art in indicators based on administrative data. Subsequent to the development of the AHRQ QIs under the EPC contract, AHRQ contracted with Stanford University for the Support for Quality Indicators (SQI) contract. Subcontractors on the contract included UC Davis and Battelle Memorial Institute. This was followed by a contract with Battelle Memorial Institute for the Support for Quality Indicators II (SQI-II) contract. Subcontractors on the contract included UC Davis and Stanford University. The purpose of both contracts was to provide a bridge between the developers and the users of the measures. The contracts included provisions for technical support, updating of indicators and modules, development of user friendly tools and documentation etc. The goals of the contract were to help demonstrate and increase the value of the QIs, respond to user needs, and increase the use of measures derived from administrative data in quality improvement and public reporting efforts. This work continued to be supported by Battelle Memorial Institute under the Support for Quality Indicators III (SQI-III) contract. This next phase of the AHRQ QI Maintenance contract will focus solely on maintaining and supporting the use of the AHRQ QIs in health care reform, transitioning the AHRQ QIs to the EHR and ICD-10 environment, and provide necessary technical assistance. Methodological improvements and enhancements requiring analytic and/or substantive expertise will be conducted by AHRQ under separate mechanisms and shared as appropriate and when needed with the AHRQ QI contractor. C.1.1.3 Characteristics and Uses of the Current AHRQ QIs The nature and the uses of the QIs have evolved considerably during the past decades. The original HCUP Quality Indicators were primarily used for quality improvement efforts and were based upon a "common denominator" hospital discharge data set, (i.e. using administrative data elements collected by all or nearly all hospital data systems through the Uniform Bill) (CMS, Medicare Claims Processing Manual, 2013). The current AHRQ QIs take advantage of data enhancements, improvements in coding practices and advanced statistical methodologies where possible,. New data elements for which the evidence suggests inclusion in the measure specifications improves the accuracy and the reliability of the measures, such as Present-onAdmission (POA) and laboratory values, have been incorporated. Furthermore, the original HCUP Quality Indicators, which were used primarily for research and quality improvement,
Page 12 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

were conceived as fairly rudimentary measures that indicated where quality problems may exist simply signaling a need for further investigation. As such, the original HCUP Quality Indicators did not require sophisticated programming knowledge, rigorous precision, or risk adjustment for comparisons across hospital performance. Over time, the AHRQ QIs evolved into metrics used not only for quality improvement but also into metrics for public reporting and pay-forperformance. The current AHRQ QIs are complex measures with sophisticated risk adjustment methodology and applications for missing data. As a result of this evolution, the uses and the users of the AHRQ QIs are much more extensive and diverse than in the early years. The AHRQ QIs now serve multiple purposes, including research, needs assessments for planning at the local, state, and national levels, hospital and community quality improvement initiatives, performance assessment through private and public reporting, public reporting to reward favorable outcomes and encourage changes in hospital behavior, and information to be used by healthcare purchasers that link performance with payment. Users of the AHRQ QIs vary and include researchers, State data organizations, community health organizations, hospitals and hospital systems, hospital associations, State Medicaid agencies, Centers for Medicare & Medicaid Services (CMS), large private purchasers and public-private purchaser coalitions, consumer groups, and local, State and Federal health care planning and monitoring organizations. State hospital associations, state data associations, hospitals and hospital systems use AHRQ QIs to help them understand how their hospital(s) compares on various quality dimensions in relationship to other hospitals, the state, or national average. Many of these organizations use the AHRQ QIs in hospital-level reporting programs. Researchers, quality improvement officers, hospital administrators and community health planners use AHRQ QIs to ask questions that provide initial feedback about clinical areas appropriate for further, more in-depth analysis, such as: What can the pediatric AHRQ Prevention QIs tell me about the adequacy of pediatric primary care in my community? How does the hysterectomy rate in our area compare with the national average? State hospital associations can use the AHRQ QIs to do quick screens of hospital quality and primary care access. Hospitals and hospital systems nationwide can use the AHRQ QIs in quality improvement initiatives where the QIs are used to identify a potential quality problem, interventions are put in place and the QIs are used to determine whether the intervention had an impact. Managed care organizations, business-health coalitions, and others are poised to begin assessments using hospital discharge data to answer questions such as: Can we design community interventions to reduce hospital admission rates in areas with high rates of diabetes complications? Which AHRQ QIs can be incorporated into performance management initiatives for our member hospitals? Federal and State policymakers use the AHRQ QIs to track health care quality in the United States over time and to assess whether health care quality is improving, for example: How does the rate of coronary artery bypass graft procedures vary over time and across regions of the United States? Another such example is the use of the AHRQ QIs to track national health care
Page 13 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

quality and disparities in the Congressionally-mandated National Healthcare Quality and Disparities Reports. In addition to the examples above, the AHRQ QIs are currently being used in hospital-level public reporting programs in 32 states affecting more than three-fourths of the US population. The AHRQ QIs are included in My Own Network powered by AHRQ (MONAHRQŽ) and on the Center for Medicare & Medicaid Services (CMS)' Hospital Compare website in 2011; have been included in the methodology used for U.S. News and World Reports ranking of the top 100 hospitals in the US; are part of the Leapfrog Safety Score, and Partnership for Patients; and are used by numerous hospitals and hospital systems in their quality improvement programs. The AHRQ QIs are supportive of several initiatives called for under the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act (ACA) Public Law 111-148, including activities related to Section 3001, Section 3008, and Section 10303.

C.1.1.4. Overview of the AHRQ QI Modules C.1.1.4.1. General Overview The AHRQ QIs currently consist of four modules of indicators of the quality of care. The AHRQ QI modules are: Prevention Quality Indictors (PQIs) or ambulatory care sensitive conditions that identify adult hospital admissions that evidence suggests could have been avoided, at least in part, through high-quality outpatient care. Initially released in November of 2000, there are currently 17 PQIs, including 14 area-level indicators and 3 composite indicators. The PQIs are population based and adjusted for gender and age. Even though these indicators are based on hospital inpatient data, they provide insight into the community health care system or services outside the hospital setting. For example, patients with diabetes may be hospitalized for diabetic complications if their conditions are not adequately monitored or if they do not receive the patient education needed for appropriate selfmanagement. The PQIs can be used as a "screening tool" to help flag potential health care quality problem areas that need further investigation; provide a quick check on primary care access or outpatient services in a community by using patient data found in a typical hospital discharge abstract; and, help public health agencies, State data organizations, health care systems, and others interested in improving health care quality in their communities. With high-quality, community-based primary care, hospitalization for these illnesses often can be avoided. Although other factors outside the direct control of the health care system, such as poor environmental conditions or lack of patient adherence to treatment recommendations, can result in hospitalization, the PQIs provide a good starting point for assessing quality of health services in the community. Because the PQIs are calculated using readily available hospital administrative data, they are an easy-to-use and inexpensive screening tool. They can be used to provide a window into the community -- to identify unmet community health care needs, to
Page 14 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

monitor how well complications from a number of common conditions are being avoided in the outpatient setting, and to compare performance of local health care systems across communities. Inpatient Quality Indicators (IQIs) reflect quality of care for adults inside hospitals and include: Inpatient mortality for medical conditions; inpatient mortality for surgical procedures; utilization of procedures for which there are questions of overuse, underuse, or misuse; and volume of procedures for which there is evidence that a higher volume of procedures maybe associated with lower mortality. Initially released in May of 2002, there are currently 34 IQIs, including 6 volume indicators at the provider-level, 15 risk-adjusted provider-level indicators, 7 provider-level indicators that are not risk-adjusted, 4 area-level indicators, and 2 composite indicators. Risk adjustment of the current IQIs include: patient demographics, the 3M APRDRGs, MDC, transfer status, point-of-origin, and procedure days, when available. Additionally, 5 of the indicators include risk stratification. The IQIs can be used to help hospitals identify potential problem areas that might need further study; provide the opportunity to assess quality of care inside the hospital using administrative data found in the typical discharge record; include mortality indicators for conditions or procedures for which mortality can vary from hospital to hospital; include utilization indicators for procedures for which utilization varies across hospitals or geographic areas; and, include volume indicators for procedures for which outcomes may be related to the volume of those procedures performed. Patient Safety Indicators (PSIs) also reflect quality of care for adults inside hospitals, but focus on potentially avoidable complications and iatrogenic events. Initially released in March of 2003, there are currently 26 PSIs, including 12 risk-adjusted provider-level indicators, 6 provider-level indicators that are not risk-adjusted, 7 area-level indicators and 1 composite indicator. Risk adjustment of the current PSIs include: age, modified-DRG, MDC, comorbidities, transfer status, point-of-origin, and procedure days, if available. In addition, one of the indicators contains specifications for risk stratification. These indicators are some of the most frequently used indicators in quality improvement and public reporting initiatives. The PSIs can be used to help hospitals identify potential adverse events that might need further study; provide the opportunity to assess the incidence of adverse events and in hospital complications using administrative data found in the typical discharge record; include indicators for complications occurring in hospitals that may represent patient safety events; and, indicators also have area level analogs designed to detect patient safety events on a regional level. Pediatric Quality Indicators (PDIs) both reflect quality of care for children below the age of 18 and neonates inside hospitals and identify potentially avoidable hospitalizations among children. The PDIs focus on potentially preventable complications and iatrogenic events for pediatric patients treated in hospitals, and on preventable hospitalizations among pediatric patients (i.e., a combination of IQIs, PSIs and PQIs for the pediatric population). Initially released in April of 2006, there are currently 21 PDIs, including 9 risk-adjusted provider-level indicators, 3 providerlevel indicators that are not risk adjusted, 5 area-level indicators, and 4 composite indicators. Risk adjustment models for the current PDIs may include: age, gender, birth weight, Modified DRG, MDC, Clinical Classification Software groupings of conditions, transfer status, point of origin and procedure days. In addition, some of the indicators have risk-stratified categories.

Page 15 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

The PDIs apply to the special characteristics of the pediatric population; screen for problems that pediatric patients experience as a result of exposure to the healthcare system and that may be amenable to prevention by changes at the provider level or area level; and, help to evaluate preventive care for children in an outpatient setting, and most children are rarely hospitalized. The AHRQ QIs rely on data elements and standard coding practices found within billing or claims data, as provided on the Uniform Bill (UB-04) (http://www.nubc.org/). The National Uniform Billing Committee (NUBC) and the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention's National Center for Health Statistics provides guidance on the standard coding of diagnoses and procedures (e.g., http://www.cdc.gov/nchs/icd/icd9.htm and http://www.cdc.gov/nchs/icd/icd10.htm ), Present-on-Admission indication of conditions and external cause of injury, patient demographics (gender, race/ethnicity), expected payer, admission status and point-of-origin, and discharge disposition. All of the technical specifications and methodology used to develop the AHRQ QIs can be found on the AHRQ QI website: www.qualityindicators.ahrq.gov ` C.1.1.4.2 Methodology Overview The current empirical methods of the AHRQ QIs are described in a comprehensive AHRQ QI report entitled, Quality Indicator Empirical Methods, posted on the AHRQ QI website (www.qualityindicators.ahrq.gov). An indicator may describe results at the provider or hospital-level or at an area or populationlevel. Some indicators indicate the number of times a medical procedure was performed within a facility (volume indicators). Most indicators, however, are expressed as ratios with a numerator of counts of hospitalizations with a condition or outcome of interest (e.g., mortality) and a denominator of the population at risk (e.g., discharges or census population estimates). The AHRQ QI software calculates several rates, including the observed rate in the population of interest, the expected rate based on the reference (nearly universal) population, the riskadjusted rate that accounts for the mix of patients in the hospital, and the smoothed rate (or performance score) that accounts for some of the noise in the data. The risk adjustment of the AHRQ QI modules varies by the type of indicator. Provider-level indicators are risk-adjusted by deriving a hierarchical logic regression model that uses a generalized estimating equations (GEE) approach to account for clustering at the provider level. When GEE models do not converge, a multivariable logistic regression model that does not account for correlation is employed. Area-level indicators are risk adjusted for age and gender, and optionally for poverty. Smoothed rates (or performance scores) are the weighted average of the risk-adjusted rates and the reference population rates (or a regression determined target estimate based on provider attributes such as volume or prior performance). The smoothed rate is derived using empirical Bayes shrinkage to result in a rate that minimizes noise from the estimates. The shrinkage estimator is based on signal and noise variance estimates. When there is a fixed comparative rate

Page 16 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

of interest, it is possible to parameterize the smoothed rates posterior probability distribution to assign performance categories for public reporting. Additionally, the AHRQ QI modules have several composite indicators that combine the results of several indicators. The AHRQ IQI composites are derived from the weighted average of the observed-to-expected ratios for a set of component AHRQ IQIs. The weights include component weights and shrinkage weights. The component weights are denominator weights, defined as the relative frequency of the denominators for the component indicators in the reference population. The shrinkage weights are the signal-to-noise ratio, where the signal variance is estimated from the reference population, and the noise variance is estimated from the user's data and is unique to each provider's user's data. The AHRQ PSI and a PDI composite is derived from the weighted average of the observed-to-expected ratios for a set of AHRQ PSIs. The weights include component weights and shrinkage weights. The component weights are numerator weights, defined as the relative frequency of the numerators for the component indicators in the reference population. The shrinkage weights are the signal-to-noise ratio, where the signal variance is estimated from the reference population, and the noise variance is estimated form the user's data and is unique to each provider in the user's data. The AHRQ PQI composites are expressed as rates based on a numerator of the aggregate count of hospitalizations for each component indicator and a denominator of the population at risk (e.g., census population estimates). C.1.1.5. Description of AHRQ QI Documentation Documentation is a cornerstone feature of the AHRQ QI program, as it serves as the primary resource for the AHRQ QI algorithms and how the AHRQ QIs were created. Documentation includes the technical specifications of each of the AHRQ QIs, technical reports that support the AHRQ QIs, software manuals, detailed descriptions of the AHRQ QI measures including numerators, denominators, reliability and validity estimates, and interpretation of rates, reports that describe how and why measures were developed, benchmarks of AHRQ QI rates/ratios, and lists of publications that use the AHRQ QIs. Ideally, documentation is created and updated in tandem with the creation of each version of the AHRQ QIs and as necessary to correct documentation errors noted by users. C.1.1.6. Description of AHRQ QI Software The AHRQ QI program is unique in that it provides publicly available software tools that calculate the AHRQ QI rates. This software legally resides in the public domain and is available as a free download from the AHRQ QI website. It allows users to calculate QI measure results based on their own administrative data set using a standard desktop computer. The AHRQ QI software tools are a key part of AHRQ's QI program. They help to support AHRQ's objective to disseminate standardized, transparent quality measures; to ensure that the measures are calculated in a consistent and accurate manner; and to remove barriers to their use. The AHRQ QI software allows users to calculate QI rates based on their own administrative data using a standard desktop computer. Each version of the QIs is released with software in two different platforms: a SASŽ application and a Windows application. The SAS software was first released in the late 1990s. It consists of several modules of SAS code and requires a SAS
Page 17 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

license to run (http://www.qualityindicators.ahrq.gov/software/SAS.aspx) . The Windows-based software, known as WinQI, was first released in 2005 (http://www.qualityindicators.ahrq.gov/software/winQI.aspx ). It was created in order to provi e an easy-to-use, low-cost option for calculating the QIs that was not dependent on licensed software. Developed on MicrosoftŽ Visual Studio using C# and .NET, AHRQ WinQI runs on Windows operating systems and requires only freely available software components: AHRQ-produced software, Microsoft .NET (for runtime environment and core software libraries), and Microsoft SQL Server Express (for data storage and manipulation). Table 1 lists the differences between SAS QI v4.5 and WinQI v4.5. Both the SAS and WinQI software are available in Version 4.5 as either 32-bit or 64-bit applications. The 32-bit applications are targeted for Windows XP operating systems, and the 64-bit applications are targeted for Windows 7 operating systems. Both versions of the QI software are updated on an annual basis to reflect changes in the AHRQ QI technical specifications. New software versions and updated technical specifications are released simultaneously. Routine annual updates include yearly International Classification of Diseases, 9th revision, Clinical Modification (ICD-9-CM), Medicare severity-Diagnostic Related Groups (MS-DRG), Major Diagnostic Categories (MDC), updated version of the 3MTM APR-DRG grouper, new Census population files, newly derived risk adjustment parameters, and an updated prediction module to account for missing POA data associated with covariates (see Sections C.6). As the AHRQ QIs have matured and their use has grown, the AHRQ QI software tools have needed to transition from small research prototypes to widely used, production quality software. This growth in volume, visibility, and use has brought a significant need for software sophistication and support. AHRQ's priorities regarding the software tools include:  ensuring that they remain an accessible, low cost, and user friendly way to use the AHRQ QIs  updating and expanding the tools to keep pace with changes in the QIs  supporting software users with well-organized, accurate documentation and responsive technical assistance  employing good software development and testing practices that ensure timely, accurate, and robust software products C.1.1.7. The web site provides detailed information on each of the QI modules, the specifications and methodology for the measures, as well as the SAS and windows-based software which calculates the QI rates. Assess validity, reliability, feasibility and/or acceptability of proposed refinements to the technical specifications** 6. Update technical specifications 7. Calculate risk adjusted models and estimate risk adjusted parameter estimates**

C.1.2. National Initiatives associated with the AHRQ Quality Indicators C.1.2.1. National Quality Forum (NQF) Endorsement The National Quality Forum (NQF) is a private, not-for-profit membership organization with more than 375 members representing multiple sectors of the healthcare system. NQF operates under a three-part mission to improve the quality of American healthcare by:  Setting national priorities and goals for performance improvement.
 Endorsing national consensus standards for measuring and publicly reporting on

performance.
 Promoting the attainment of national goals through education and outreach programs.

NQF is a voluntary consensus standards setting body as specified by the National Technology and Transfer Advancement Act of 1995 and OMB Circular A-119 (1998). NQF endorsement, which involves rigorous, evidence-based review and a formal Consensus Development Process, has become the "gold standard" for healthcare performance measures. Major healthcare purchasers, including CMS, rely on NQF-endorsed measures to ensure that the measures are scientifically sound and meaningful and to help standardize performance measures used across the industry. To date, NQF has endorsed more than 600 measures; among them are over 40 AHRQ QIs. In 2007, AHRQ submitted a set of AHRQ QIs to the National Quality Forum (NQF) for endorsement under their consensus development process. That effort resulted in the successful endorsement of 47 AHRQ QIs for public reporting: 15 IQIs, 9 PSIs, 11 PDIs, 12 PQIs (Appendix 1:NQF Endorsed Individual and Composite Measures). This facilitated an increase in the use of the AHRQ QIs for hospital-level public reporting by CMS and many states. Currently, there are 32 states which publicly report some or all of the AHRQ QIs (see Appendix 2 and Appendix 3).
Page 20 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

C.1.2.2. THE HEALTHCARE COST AND UTILIZATION PROJECT (HCUP) The AHRQ QIs measures and tools are annually updated and tested using data from HCUP (www.hcup-us.ahrq.gov). HCUP (pronounced "H-Cup") is a family of health care databases and related software tools, products, and services developed through a Federal-State-Industry partnership and sponsored by AHRQ. HCUP brings together the data collection efforts of State data organizations, hospital associations, private data organizations, and the Federal government to create a national health care information resource of patient-level health care data (HCUP Partners, http://www.hcup-us.ahrq.gov/partners.jsp?SID). HCUP includes the largest collection of longitudinal hospital care data in the United States, with all-payer, encounter level information beginning in 1988. These databases enable research on a broad range of health policy issues, including cost and quality of health services, medical practice patterns, access to health care programs and outcomes of treatments at the national State and local market levels. The core of HCUP is a unique data source that provides record-level data on health care utilization for a range of settings including acute inpatient, emergency department, and ambulatory surgery. The information provided in HCUP spans patient demographics, ICD-9CM diagnoses and procedures and Healthcare Common Procedure Coding System/Current Procedural Terminology (HCPCS/CPT) procedures, how the patient was admitted and discharged, and resource utilization. Beginning with the 2014 data, diagnoses and procedures will be coded with ICD-10-CM and ICD-10-Procedure Coding System. HCUP is unique because it collects all records in all settings from those data sources that participate. It is also unique because of the derivative databases that can be used to generate national estimates. The HCUP databases are available for intramural research within AHRQ. Restricted access public release versions of many of the HCUP databases are available to the public through the AHRQ-sponsored HCUP Central Distributor. The content of the restricted access public release versions of the HCUP databases is developed in partnership with the participating data organizations. Because the participating data organizations dictate the release of specific data elements, the data elements on the restricted access public release databases are a subset of the data elements in the intramural databases. The HCUP databases are available to users who sign a Data Use Agreement (DUA). This agreement allows the data to be used for research and aggregate statistical reporting, but prohibits the identification of individual hospitals in disseminated materials. The HCUP family of health services databases includes:  HCUP state databases ­ state-specific annual inpatient and outpatient data files including the State Inpatient Databases (SID), State Ambulatory Surgery Databases (SASD) and State Emergency Department Databases (SEDD) HCUP nationwide databases ­ nationwide samples derived from the HCUP state databases enabling focused research on special populations including the Nationwide Inpatient Sample (NIS), Kids' Inpatient Database (KID) and the Nationwide Emergency Department Sample (NEDS)



Page 21 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV



Other HCUP-related databases and tools ­ supplementary databases and tools that are used to augment information in the HCUP state and nationwide databases including the Clinical Classification Software (CCS) for ICD-9-CM, Comorbidity Software (http://www.hcup-us.ahrq.gov/tools_software.jsp)

There are currently 47 organizations within the HCUP Partnership, all of whom have given permission for the AHRQ QI program to use their data for development of AHRQ QIs and AHRQ QI software. To date, AHRQ QIs have been developed using the HCUP SID, NIS and KID, although future AHRQ QI modules may rely on the SEDD, NEDS, and SASD. Additional information about HCUP can be found at http://www.hcup-us.ahrq.gov. C.1.2.3. NATIONAL QUALITY MEASURES CLEARINGHOUSE (NQMC) NQMC, an AHRQ-supported initiative for DHHS, is a database and Web site for information on specific evidence-based health care quality measures and measure sets. NQMC seeks to provide practitioners, health care providers, health plans, integrated delivery systems, purchasers and others an accessible mechanism for obtaining detailed information on quality measures, and to further their dissemination, implementation, and use in order to inform health care decision and promote widespread access to quality measures by the health care community and other interested individuals. NQMC has been available as a public resource since February 2003. The AHRQ QIs are included in the NQMC. More information on the NQMC can be found at: http://www.qualitymeasures.ahrq.gov/index.aspx. C.1.2.4. NATIONAL HEALTHCARE QUALITY AND DISPARITIES REPORTS Every year since 2003, AHRQ has worked collaboratively with DHHS agencies and reported to the US Congress on the progress and opportunities for improving health care quality and reducing health care disparities. As mandated by the Healthcare Research and Quality Act of 1999 (Public Law 106-129), the National Healthcare Quality Report (NHQR) focuses on "national trends in the quality of health care provided to the American people" (42 USC 299b2(b)(2)). The National Healthcare Disparities Report (NHDR) focuses on "prevailing disparities in health care delivery as it relates to racial factors and socioeconomic factors in priority populations" (42 USC 299a-1(a)(6)). The NHQR and NHDR include a broad set of performance measures including most of the AHRQ QIs. While initially intended for legislative policy makers, the reports have grown in scope and importance to a variety of national and international audiences (IOM, 2010). More information on the NHQR and NHDR can be found at: http://www.ahrq.gov/research/findings/nhqrdr/index.html C.1.2.5. MY OWN NETWORK, POWERED BY AHRQ (MONAHRQ) MONAHRQ is a desktop software tool created by AHRQ that enables organizations ­ such as state and local data organizations, regional reporting collaboratives, hospitals and hospital systems, and health plans ­ to quickly and easily generate a health care reporting Website. MONAHRQ analyzes, summarizes, and presents information in a format ready for use by consumers and other decision makers on: quality of care at the hospital level, health care utilization at the hospital level, preventable hospitalizations at the area level, rates of conditions and procedures at the area level and estimated costs and cost-savings related to quality of care. Users of MONAHRQ currently have the option of including all of the AHRQ IQIs, PSIs and

Page 22 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

PQIs. Future versions of MONAHRQ will include the PDIs. The MONAHRQ project maintains its own website and documentation at www.monahrq.ahrq.gov . C.1.2.6. CENTERS FOR MEDICARE & MEDICAID SERVICES (CMS) INITIATIVES CMS is historically the federal agency tasked with overseeing Medicare, Medicaid and Children's Health Insurance Programs (CHIP), but more recently through the ACA has been tasked with expanding access to affordable health care and making the US health care system more outcome-drive and cost-effective (e.g., ACA Section 3001, Section 3008, HR 3590 Title X, Subtitle C and provision related to Title III). CMS has used AHRQ QIs in their quality reporting programs, including the Hospital Inpatient Quality Reporting Program (IQR) and Hospital Compare. More recently, with additional mandates to coordinate with States to set up Health Insurance Marketplaces, expand Medicaid, and regulate private health insurance plans, CMS has considered the use of AHRQ QIs in other CMS programs including but not limited to Physician Quality Reporting System (PQRS), reporting by ACOs, Partnership for Patients, CMS program supporting the National Quality Strategy, programs related to the Center for Consumer Information and Insurance Oversight (CIIO), and initiatives in the Center for Medicare and Medicaid Innovation (CMMI). C.1.3. CURRENT USER DEMAND In recent years, the utilization of the AHRQ QIs has increased significantly. Approximately 200 user inquiries are received each month. There are currently 2,400 subscribers to the listserv, approximately 15,000 visits to the website monthly, and between 65,000 to 85,000 file downloads of QI documentation and software monthly. As mentioned earlier, this rapid uptake of the QIs has been due to many factors including a demand for transparent, standardized measures using readily available data, for both quality improvement and comparative reporting as well an increased focus on public reporting and pay for performance.

C.2. GOALS AND OBJECTIVES The goal of the AHRQ Quality Indicators 2014-2019 contract is to annually produce a set of AHRQ QIs that can be used to measure quality in support of research, public health planning, quality improvement initiatives and Federal, national and local public reporting programs. The objectives of this contract are to: 1) maintain scientific integrity of technical specifications and software for use in health care reform; 2) transition of the AHRQ QIs to an ICD-10 and EHR environment, and 3) provide customer-oriented technical assistance to users of the AHRQ QIs.

This report shall be similar in nature to the Annual Report, but also include overall summaries and recommendations from the perspective of the life of the contract. Return of all data and software must be documented.

C.6. COORDINATE THE MAINTENANCE, TESTING, AND RELEASE OF ALL AHRQ QI MODULES The AHRQ QI Initiative can be broadly divided into 2 categories: development and maintenance. This statement of work focuses on the latter. Maintenance activities are primarily those tasks which update the software and documentation based on coding updates, new data elements and/or implementation of select user-suggested improvements. Changes to AHRQ QI methodology (both clinical and statistical) are not part of this requirement. Such changes shall be provided by AHRQ for implementation during the regular maintenance cycle. C.6.1. COORDINATION AND PLANNING FOR THE MAINTENANCE AND TESTING OF ALL AHRQ QI MODULES The maintenance and testing of the AHRQ QI modules are keys to the success of this project. The Contractor shall develop and carry out a coordinated plan for the maintenance and testing of the modules. The Contractor shall provide a dedicated team member to be accountable for the coordinated maintenance and testing of all of the AHRQ QI modules. The Contractor shall assume a 12-month maintenance cycle. The Contractor shall communicate about coordination activities so that the AHRQ COR is kept abreast of various issues that arise during the course of the project. C.6.2. CENSUS DATA OR OTHER POPULATION-BASED ESTIMATES DATA The AHRQ QI area level measures use the population of the county of the patient's residence as the denominator. The population counts are provided by the US Census Bureau Population Estimates program. In the past, the data required manipulation to be able to have appropriate area level estimates. There is typically a two year lag in the population estimates. AHRQ will provide data for population-based estimates. C.6.3. CREATE REFERENCE POPULATION FILES FROM HCUP DATA The annual creation of the reference population files serve to produce the expected rates to reflect a given time period of the data. For instance, the reference population file that appears in version 4.5 of the AHRQ QIs are based on 2010 HCUP data from 44 HCUP-participating states. The Contractor shall be prepared to annually update such a reference population should AHRQ pursue this route in version 4.6 and future versions. The Contractor shall work with AHRQ to determine the earliest possible time at which the reference population data may be available, coordinating with the COR during the first quarter of the fiscal year. The data set used for the work related to the current four AHRQ QI modules is the Healthcare Cost and Utilization Project (HCUP) State Inpatient Data (SID). Currently, the SID represents 47 States of all-payer inpatient data sets, which represents approximately 97% of admissions in the US. Note that there are AHRQ QI modules under development that use other HCUP data sets, such as the AHRQ emergency department community-level indicators which will use HCUP State Emergency Department Databases and/or Nationwide Emergency Department Sample. It is necessary for the Contractor to have adequate knowledge, experience, hardware, and software technology to regularly work with such data.

Page 30 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

Additionally, annually, the Contractor shall work with the COR to identify any changes to the processing and format of the reference population data (HCUP) (e.g., format of the data elements). C.6.4. UPDATE TOOLS REQUIRED TO MAINTAIN, TEST AND IMPROVE THE AHRQ QI MODULES The reference population to date has consistently had an 18-month time lag. However, it is important to note that the AHRQ QI software is a tool that some Users will run on real-time data. Therefore, the codes and groupers embedded in the AHRQ QI modules going forward shall be the most recent available. In addition, the Contractor shall confirm that all codes and groupers are backward compatible through previous data years. C.6.4.1. REVIEW CHANGES IN ADMINISTRATIVE DATA ELEMENTS, CODING AND GUIDELINES Occasionally, there are changes introduced to existing fields of administrative data. For example, a code for "admitted from hospice care" in the Point of Origin field was added January 2010 to the hospital administrative data. Consideration needs to be given to the applicability of such changes to the AHRQ QIs. The Contractor shall review all coding guidelines and considerations from the National Uniform Billing Committee and create a list of changes annually as it applies to the AHRQ QIs. The Contractor shall work with the COR to identify any data related changes to the processing and format of the reference population data (HCUP). The Contractor shall review all annual changes and updates to national coding systems and coding practice guidelines. ICD and MS-DRG updates are usually made available on August 1 of every year and go into effect on October 1 for the following year (e.g., FY 2013 changes went into effect October 1, 2012). In addition, the Contractor should expect that annual coding changes may occur with ICD-9, ICD-10-CM, ICD-10-PCS, and HCPCS/CPT schema. The Contractor shall be knowledgeable of, and anticipate changes in, ICD-9, ICD-10 and CPT-4 codes, the CMS submission forms, such as the UB04 and its successor. In addition, the Contractor shall implement a process which can be flexible enough to integrate changes not yet anticipated without creating undue burden on resources. The Contractor shall proactively compile a list of all coding updates to the AHRQ QI modules by the end of the previous fiscal year. The Contractor shall make recommendations to AHRQ regarding administrative coding as it relates to the AHRQ QIs. C.6.4.2. ICD-10-CM/ICD-10-PCS SET NAMES, TESTING, AND CODING UPDATES It is expected that beginning October 1, 2014, ICD-10-CM and ICD-10-PCS will become the new standard for coding diagnoses and procedures in hospital discharge data. The Contractor shall re-specify and test all measures in AHRQ QI modules in accordance with the guidance provided by the Code Maintenance Project Panel convened by the NQF (NQF, 2010). The majority of conversions to ICD-10 coded AHRQ QI modules will have been completed prior to this contract (Cox and Romano, July 2013; Center for Health Policy, 2011), with each of the indicators having a corresponding set of ICD-10-CM/PCS codes that match the clinical intent of the ICD-9-CM coded modules. Annual updates will be accomplished under this task. In
Page 31 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

addition, revisions to ICD-10-coded AHRQ QI modules will be made if necessary because of incorrect code assignment during the conversion process. Beginning October, 2014, the Contractor shall support both ICD-9 and ICD-10-CM/PCS versions of the AHRQ QI software (in both SAS and Windows implementations). Initially, the ICD-10-CM/PCS version of the AHRQ QI will not include a reference population, as no database is available. The Contractor is expected to provide the documentation and software that include ICD-10-CM/PCS coding of numerators and denominators. The Contractor will be provided a small dual-coded database to test the integrity of the software. The Contractor will be expected to use ICD-10-CM/PCS-coded HCUP databases to serve as a reference population, beginning when 2015 ICD-10-CM/PCS databases are available. The Contractor shall ensure that both versions are fully tested, accurate, and developed using good quality control metrics. The contractor shall provide full documentation for both versions, updating the QI site so as to make both versions and their documentation easily available and usable. The Contractor shall provide full technical support for both versions, consistent with the requirements of section C.9 below. Technical support shall include logging and summarizing for AHRQ all concerns, issues, and feedback received concerning the new versions. The contractor shall provide additional documentation, technical assistance, and dissemination activities that publicize the new versions, explain differences between the old and new versions, and assist QI software users in transitioning from one version to the other. The Contractor shall evaluate any questions raised and/or issues that occurred to recommend to AHRQ whether the transition to ICD-10CM/PCS warranted maintaining or dropping the ICD9 versions of the software tools. By December 2016, the decision to support only the ICD-10CM/PCS versions shall be made by the AHRQ COR. Barring any significant problems with the new versions and concerns raised by the user community, it is anticipated that ICD-9-CM versions shall no longer be updated and maintained past 2015 data year, although the contractor shall continue to make them available in QI Web archives and provide their users, if any, with technical support as necessary. The Contractor shall routinely compile a list of all coding updates to the AHRQ QI modules by September 30th of each year. C.6.4.3. GROUPER SOFTWARE AND CLASSIFICATION SCHEME UPDATES A number of clinical grouping methodologies in addition to MS-DRGs and MDCs are employed in the AHRQ QIs in various capacities, such as identifying denominator exclusions, and risk adjustment. The primary grouping methodologies that are used, or could be proposed to be used, in the AHRQ QIs include but are not limited to:   Comorbidity Software (http://www.hcupus.ahrq.gov/toolssoftware/comorbidity/comorbidity.jsp) Clinical Classifications Software for ICD-9-CM (http://www.hcupus.ahrq.gov/toolssoftware/ccs/ccs.jsp)

Page 32 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

 

  





Clinical Classifications Software for ICD-10 (http://www.hcupus.ahrq.gov/toolssoftware/icd_10/ccs_icd_10.jsp) CMS Hierarchical Condition Categories (HCC) Risk Adjustment (Pope et al., 2011) (http://www.cms.gov/Medicare/Health-Plans/MedicareAdvtgSpecRateStats/RiskAdjustors.html) Medicare Severity-Diagnostic Related Groups (MS-DRGs) Modified DRGs as described in the Empirical Methods Document ­ to be provided by AHRQ Major Diagnostic Categories (MDC) and CMS Medicare Severity Diagnosis Related Grouper available through the National Technical Information Service (NTIS) http://www.ntis.gov/products/grouper.aspx 3M All Patient Refined Diagnosis Related Groups (APR-DRGs) (http://solutions.3m.com/wps/portal/3M/en_US/3M_Health_Information_Systems/HIS/Pr oducts/APRDRG_Software/) RACHS-1(http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/15283367)

The above grouping methodologies are maintained by arrangements outside of this contract with the exception of Modified DRGs. The Modified DRGs are essentially a cross-walking and collapsing of related MS-DRGs and CMS-DRGs for the construction of groupings used in risk adjustment. The Modified DRGs shall be annually reviewed and updated by the Contractor, and consideration should be given as to whether the Modified DRG should continue to be used with the AHRQ QIs. The Contractor shall coordinate updates on 3M APR-DRGs with AHRQ and with 3M staff. The Contractor shall be aware of standing limited license agreements and work collaboratively with 3M to get the most recent updates. Updates from 3M include software that is 32-bit and 64bit compatible that can stand alone or be embedded within AHRQ WinQI software. In regard to the other grouping methodologies, the Contractor shall annually review and analyze them for revisions and the appropriate integration into the AHRQ QIs. RACHS-1 is only used in the risk adjustment for pediatric heart surgery mortality measure (PDI 6). The Contractor shall facilitate the necessary agreements between AHRQ and Children's Hospital in Boston to regularly receive updates of RACHS-1. The Contractor shall routinely compile a list of all grouping updates, including but not limited to coding updates, changes in the version of software or software requirements, that may relate to the AHRQ QI modules by September 30th of each year. AHRQ also has a co-stewardship agreement with National Perinatal Information Center for Birth Trauma Rate ­ Injury to Neonate (previously NQF endorsed 0474) (AHRQ PSI 17).

C.6.5 CREATE SAS SOFTWARE MODULE AND SPECIFICATIONS FOR WINDOWS-BASED SOFTWARE TOOL The technical specifications of the AHRQ QIs are conveyed not only through documentation but also by the creation of SAS software programs. These programs while the basis of development of the AHRQ QIs; serve as a mechanism of transparency to convey to users how the AHRQ QIs are created and as an application for AHRQ QI users to calculate their own rates. The Contractor shall update and deliver all SAS programs on an annual basis and on a periodic basis, as requested by the COR. Updates to the software are based on updates to the technical specifications of the AHRQ QIs as well as required for the calculation of risk adjusted, risk stratified and smoothed rates. Previous versions of the AHRQ QI software handle missing Present on Admission (POA) data using a prediction module based on the Markov Chain Monte Carlo method (as described in the Empirical Methods Report). Going forward, the prediction module will no longer be supported by AHRQ. The Contractor is not expected to maintain or support the prediction module in future versions. In addition, the Contractor may be asked to stream-line and add technical clarification comments so that the SAS programs run as efficiently as possible. The Contractor shall provide a SAS QI instruction manual (reviewed and approved by AHRQ) with each release of the AHRQ QIs. Periodically, the Contractor will be asked to deliver supplemental SAS programs to meet the needs of external users of the AHRQ QIs, as identified in Section C.15. Beginning with Version 4.1, the AHRQ QI SAS software does not support weighted QI estimates or standard errors for weighted estimates. Future versions of the AHRQ QI software shall enable the calculation of weighted QI estimates from complex sampling designs. AHRQ will provide the necessary methodological and analytic information to implement these and other substantial updates to future versions of the AHRQ QI software. This may include, but is not

C.7. MAINTAIN AND REFINE AHRQ QI SOFTWARE TOOLS The AHRQ QI software allows users to calculate QI rates based on their own administrative data using a standard desktop computer. Each version of the QIs is released with software in two different platforms: a SASŽ application and a Windows application. The SAS software, while used to develop the AHRQ QIs, is also disseminated to AHRQ QI users. The WinQI software is geared only for AHRQ QI users, as an easy-to-use, low-cost resource for calculating the QIs that was not dependent on licensed software. C.7.1 CREATE, MAINTAIN AND UPDATE WINDOWS-BASED QI (WINQI) SOFTWARE TOOL The AHRQ QI initiative includes a Windows-based software program that was created in order to provide an easy-to-use, low-cost option for calculating the QIs that was not dependent on licensed software. Developed on MicrosoftŽ Visual Studio using C# and .NET, AHRQ WinQI runs on Windows operating systems and requires only freely available software components: AHRQ-produced software, Microsoft .NET (for runtime environment and core software libraries), and Microsoft SQL Server Express (for data storage and manipulation). Beginning in Version 4.5, the WinQI software is available in 32-bit or 64-bit applications. The 32-bit applications are targeted for Windows XP operating systems, and the 64-bit applications are targeted for Windows 7 operating systems. The creation, maintenance and testing of the Windows-based AHRQ QI software requires the Contractor to engage expert software developers with extensive knowledge of C#, .NET, SQL and the ability to read and understand SAS, effective communication, organization, coordination across various tasks, and detailed documentation within the project. C.7.1.1. Provide Quality Assurances for WinQI Software The Contractor shall establish and adhere to a high level of software development, testing, documentation and quality control standards and procedures, in accordance with the AHRQ System Development Life Cycle Policy (SDLC). Also, the Contractor shall provide system administration and other database monitoring, tuning and administration software to support efficient and high quality operations of the data processing and data tasks under this contract. The Contractor shall deliver documentation of the proposed procedures and tools to be used for these functions and review the proposal with the COR for concurrence. The Contractor shall provide AHRQ with documentation templates for each component of the standard SDLC. The SDLC shall be subject to review and approval by AHRQ. The Contractor shall propose an SDLC approach that they consider appropriate for the WinQI project; the approach at a minimum shall address the following components:  Requirements analysis, including business-level requirements, technical-level requirements, requirements traceability  Design plan documentation  Implementation  Iterative testing and test plan documentation
Page 36 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

     

Iterative testing Risk mitigation Release plan documentation Release plan Maintenance plan documentation Maintenance and support, including processes for routine and non-routine maintenance releases

The Contractor shall support efficient and secure computing operations and shall utilize software workflow, document management, and other collaboration tools and products to support efficient operations of functions, processes and tasks performed under this contract. On a regular basis, the Contractor shall inventory and evaluate its interim and final products to determine whether to retain or discard products to maintain efficiency. This is henceforth referred to as "trim holdings". Prior to final closeout and accounting, the Contractor shall create a schedule for this inventory and evaluation activity and develop a plan for returning to AHRQ all the data acquired during the contract period. The contractor shall plan to trim back to the final software production programs, logs and listings, format programs and final supporting documentation as part of the final close-out procedures (see Section C.22). The Contractor shall maintain and deliver documentation of all proposed hardware, software, security, backup / recovery, networking and other Information Technology (IT) infrastructure components and solutions needed to support all AHRQ QI contractual efforts and shall obtain approval of the proposed solutions from the COR. At the direction of the COR, the Contractor shall support AHRQ in preparing responses to periodic DHHS reporting requirements for accounting for information technology (IT) activities within the AHRQ QI project. For example, DHHS requires that projects with an IT component distinguish between those costs which are Development/Modernization/Enhancement (DME) and those which are Steady State (SS) and report annually on these expenditures. The Contractor shall be able to provide support to AHRQ in assessing and reporting such information. Also, per the new Federal Information Security Management Act (FISMA) reporting requirements, the contractor will be required to periodically collect automated vulnerability scanning data and hardening configuration data for all AHRQ owned systems. The actual solution chosen to conduct vulnerability and configuration scanning is at the discretion of the contractor, but the report data must be exported in a Security Content Automation Protocol (SCAP) compliant format. Many industry leaders recognized vulnerability and configuration management tools, including Tenable Nessus, McAfee, and Symantec offer SCAP compliant products. C.7.1.2 Routine and Non-Routine Updates to the Software The Contractor shall perform routine and non-routine updates on the WinQI software on an annual basis, based on refinements and enhancements identified in Section 6. The Contractor shall make the revised software available on the AHRQ QI Website in the beginning of the second quarter of the calendar year. The Contractor shall provide a summary of all new coding changes to the COR with information about whether and how each coding change affects the
Page 37 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

indicator definitions, empirical performance, and software. The Contractor shall maintain AHRQ QI software programs from previous years and the revised programs on the AHRQ QI Web site and shall distinguish them clearly to enable users to select the programs applicable to their needs. The Contractor shall review all aspects of each software tool to ensure they are up-to-date, including programs, format libraries, online documentation and instructions. In addition, throughout the year, the Contractor shall make enhancements, revisions, and corrections to the tools based on feedback from users, as directed by the COR. The Contractor shall ensure that all software tools and their documentation are designed for the AHRQ QI Web site, with special attention to making the tools easy to use and accessible to a wide range of users. Prior to the release of all updated tools, the Contractor shall perform a thorough double check of all changes to the tools and test the tools on data to check their accuracy and to ensure that no errors have been introduced. Examples of routine updates may include, but are not limited to:           Addition and/or removal of ICD-9, ICD-10-CM/PCS, DRG, MDC codes from code lists Additional of data elements (not more than 5 per year) Renaming data elements to be consistent with UB-04 terminology (instead of HCUP data element names) Application of the most recent version of the MS-DRG Grouper software Application of most recent version of the CCS and comorbidity software Inclusion of the 3M limited license APR-DRG software Modification of inclusion/exclusion logic for numerators Modification of inclusion/exclusion logic for provider indicator denominators Modification of denominator inclusion/exclusion logic for area indicators Modification to reflect changes in coding of existing administrative data elements or addition of new data elements

Whenever problems, errors, or other issues requiring correction or modification are observed in released AHRQ QI software, data files, or other materials, the Contractor shall, at the COR's discretion, promptly resolve such issues by providing a non-routine maintenance release. The Contractor shall follow SDLC processes for these releases. Examples of non-routine but planned updates may include, but are not limited to:  Inclusion of the most recent version of the NTIS CMS DRG grouper software

C.7.2. Implement Quality Assurance Activities for SAS and WinQI Software The Contractor shall include quality assurance activities in the process to ensure creation of accurate software logic and code and reduce the likelihood of errors and inconsistencies. Additionally, as users apply the AHRQ QI software tools to their own data, potential problems in data processing and/or software logic may be uncovered, although this rarely is expected to occur. At the direction of the COR, the Contractor shall fully investigate these potential problems, report back to the COR, and rectify the problems if necessary, as directed by the COR. C.7.2.1. Audit and Review of Source Code against Documented Measure Specifications In Years 2 and 4, the Contractor shall implement an audit of the software code to ensure consistency against the measure specifications. Any differences and inconsistencies must be identified to the COR and resolved at least 4 weeks prior to external testing. The audit shall be conducted by an external subcontractor with expertise in applying the QI software to hospital discharge data. In addition to audits, software testing, such as routine testing against a constructed test suite shall be used to demonstrate that the software correctly implements the technical specifications. C.7.2.2 COMPARISON TESTING BETWEEN SAS AND WINQI SOFTWARE The Contractor shall perform testing to ensure consistency between SAS and WinQI software. Testing must be performed in the 32-bit and the 64-bit environment, using a subset of the reference population data. Testing shall include a ratio of SAS statistic over the WinQI statistic for observed numerators and denominators, observed rates, expected rates, risk-adjusted rates and smoothed rates, where applicable. In addition, testing should include a comparison of differences in numerators, denominators, observed rates, expected rates and smoothed rates at the provider-level but reported in the aggregate. All discrepancies between the SAS and WinQI software must be reviewed by the COR, with subsequent direction about that which is fixed within the software and that which is explained by the software. The most current report showing testing results between the two software platforms can be found at: http://www.qualityindicators.ahrq.gov/Downloads/Software/V45/SASWinQI%20comparison.pdf
Page 39 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

C.7.2.3 Check the Consistency in Rates with Previous Years The Contractor shall compare the AHRQ QI rates from previous years to the current year, using WinQI. The process of comparison should be similar to the one described under Section C.7.2, as directed by the COR. C.7.2.4. Apply Quality Control Edits The Contractor shall thoroughly review the measure specifications and code and make recommendations for changes, additions or deletions to improve the software code to the COR. Once reviewed and finalized, the Contractor shall incorporate these improvements into the software program. The Contractor shall review and update the list yearly for the remainder of the contract, in consultation with the COR. C.7.2.5. Provide Other Checks and Documentation Up to 4 times a contract year, the COR may request a report of additional quality control checks of the software code prior to the public release. The Contractor shall maintain a detailed and comprehensive document of quality control checks performed, status and its resolution. C.7.2.6. Comprehensive Testing Plan of QI Software The Contractor shall develop and submit to the COR a comprehensive testing plan which includes but is not limited to:     Confirms accuracy of rate calculations Assures accuracy of inclusion and exclusion criteria Confirms consistency between the SAS and Windows software versions Confirms parameter estimates are implemented correctly

The testing should be completed using at a minimum HCUP SID for the most current year available and the HCUP NIS for the most current year available. The testing plan must document the rationale for each activity described. Upon the COR's review and approval, the Contractor shall implement the testing plan each year. C.7.2.7. Document Errors in Software Versions The Contractor shall keep a log of errors requiring the re-issuing of software versions and updates. The log shall include the name of the measure affected, the type of error, where the error originated and the way it was detected, corrective steps taken to address it, action steps taken to ensure this type of error does not re-occur, and resources used to correct the error. The log shall also include the time and cost that it took to correct the error. The Contractor shall submit this log annually to the COR.

Page 40 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

C.7.3. Develop the QI Comparison Report The Contractor shall prepare a QI Comparison Report consisting of two parts. Part 1 of this report compares QI rates from the HCUP SIDs and NIS to prior years. Note that much of the report can be updated in anticipation of completing the analysis to facilitate an expedited release of the report. Part 2 of this report focuses on comparability of all QI rates between the SAS and Windows-based software. C.7.4. Release Updated Software Tools The Contractor shall develop a software release plan. This plan will include timelines and processes for ensuring communication with the user community in advance of a software release. Upon the COR's approval, the Contractor shall make the AHRQ QI software and documentation publicly available in January of each contract year. C.7.5. AHRQ Application and System Development Requirements AHRQ has implemented a Distributed Systems Engineering Lab (DSEL) to support all internal development efforts and provide the facility for housing the software and documentation for all AHRQ sponsored systems and applications, regardless of where the system or application is hosted. AHRQ uses a System Development Lifecycle (SDLC) framework which is consistent with the HHS Enterprise Lifecycle Framework (EPLC). This framework is the basis for implementation of the DSEL, conduct of development projects and the Rational Unified Process (RUP)/Capability Maturity Model (CMM) based processes that support its implementation. The SDLC framework provides a disciplined approach which employs the following traditional project phases:          Concept Initiation Planning Requirements Analysis Design Development Testing Implementation / Deployment Operations and Maintenance

Page 41 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV



Retirement

The AHRQ SDLC framework is closely aligned with the disciplines defined in the Rational Unified Process (RUP). The IBM Rational Suite of tools has been adopted by the Agency to provide a standard IT development environment for AHRQ sponsored systems and application development projects. The AHRQ SDLC framework has been enhanced through the use of tailored processes and artifacts based on the RUP methodology. The documentation deliverables required for all Information Technology (IT) projects are based on specific RUP artifacts identified by AHRQ. The Rational Clear Case libraries housed within the DSEL provide the repository for housing software and documentation artifacts related to all AHRQ sponsored systems and applications, regardless of where the system or application is hosted. Contractors are not required to follow the RUP development methodology or use the Rational Suite of tools; however, the Contractor's SDLC must be capable of producing AHRQrequired system deliverables containing the required content as described further in Appendix 5. It is required that the Contractor use the lifecycle phases defined in the AHRQ SDLC framework and obtain the COR's approval before moving from one phase to another. This approval process corresponds to the stage gates in the HHS EPLC model. The Contractor must also conform to AHRQ Configuration Management (CM) and change control standards which require appropriate controls for managing software and documentation baselines, changes to software artifacts using an appropriate IDE or version management tool, document change requests and obtaining approval through a formal change control process that requires COR and possible AHRQ IT approval prior to implementation. Appendix 5 identifies the documentation deliverables required for all IT projects and the content required for each deliverable.

C.8. DELIVERY AND MANAGEMENT OF THE AHRQ QI MODULES: TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS, SOFTWARE AND DOCUMENTATION Keeping a record of activities for AHRQ QI is a vital part of every task undertaken for the project. The Contractor shall generate documentation to accompany all AHRQ QI components (e.g., databases, software, products, and reports) as they are developed and maintain the information in an organized, consistent manner that retains institutional memory. The Contractor shall observe naming conventions and version controls strictly and the naming conventions shall be self-explanatory. Examples of AHRQ QI documentation are located at www.qualityindicators.ahrq.hhs.gov. The Contractor shall store documentation, software programs, and data files in a manner that shall protect the information while allowing appropriate access.

Page 42 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

C.8.1. Generate and Maintain Documentation The Contractor shall generate, maintain and archive all documentation products in a central internal document management system (referred to henceforth as the AHRQ QI documentation system) so as to make them readily available to users, AHRQ, or other contractors when needed. Documentation products shall be developed and maintained at a level of detail and transparency such that the activities under this contract can be replicated, audited or maintained by independent organizations. There are essentially four types of documentation that require extensive quality control and management under this contract: process documentation, methodological documentation, system documentation and user documentation. The Contractor shall develop and maintain the following types of documentation for existing and new measures and tools: 1. Process Documentation describes in detail the routine steps necessary to accomplish the task with detail as to the objectives, potential problems one may encounter and potential solutions for each step of the task. Examples of such documentation include, but are not limited to: a. Identification and determination of potential FY coding updates of ICD 9 codes, MS DRGs, etc. b. Process used to incorporate FY coding updates in software tools c. Criteria for continued maintenance of a measure versus retirement d. Quality control processes used to ensure accuracy and consistency within each product and across products e. Process for moving to ICD-10-CM/PCS coding system f. Processes for software testing, requirements tracking, and configuration management 2. Systems Documentation describes the technical aspects of the QI software and Web site. Examples of such documentation include, but are not limited to detail on: a. Systems design and architecture for QI software b. Commented source code for SAS and Windows software c. Web site map and commented source code d. FISMA documentation

Page 43 of 174

Solicitation No. 14-233-SOL-00087 Support for AHRQ Quality Indicators IV

e. Data dictionaries for databases and major data structures used by QI software f. Requirements documents 3. Methodological and User Documentation describes the technical, statistical and methodological detail, issues, caveats and considerations for each measure. Examples of such documentation include, but are not limited to detail on (see C.6.6): a. Inclusion criteria b. Exclusion criteria c. Population reference files d. Covariates e. Smoothed Rates f. Observed Rates g. Risk Adjustment h. Construction of Composite Measures i. Bibliography of all papers relevant to QI measures and updates j. Statistical programming files that include but not limited to those used for estimating risk adjustment models, producing covariates, and calculating national rates k. Release notes which inform the user on updates or corrections to publicly released products l. Software documentation m. Technical Specifications n. Log of coding changes o. Risk adjustment coefficient tables p. Comparative data tables q. Methodology Reports r. Data Dictionary
